### 41. Disease: Diabetes Mellitus (Type 2) - Variant/Sub-type 41
- **Category:** Chronic / Metabolic
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Insulin resistance and relative insulin deficiency. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Polydipsia (excessive thirst), polyuria (frequent urination), polyphagia (hunger), slow wound healing. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Fasting Plasma Glucose (FPG) >= 126 mg/dL, HbA1c >= 6.5%, Oral Glucose Tolerance Test (OGTT). Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Metformin, sulfonylureas, GLP-1 receptor agonists, insulin injections, low-carb diet, exercise. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Maintaining a healthy body weight, active lifestyle, balanced diet rich in fiber. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 42. Disease: Hypertension (High Blood Pressure) - Variant/Sub-type 42
- **Category:** Chronic / Cardiovascular
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Increased systemic vascular resistance and arterial stiffness. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Often asymptomatic (the silent killer), headache, dizziness, tinnitus, chest pressure. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Repeated blood pressure readings >= 130/80 mmHg using a sphygmomanometer. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Lifestyle changes (DASH diet, reduced sodium), ACE inhibitors, Beta-blockers, Calcium channel blockers. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Regular physical activity, reducing sodium intake, stress management, limiting alcohol. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 43. Disease: Asthma - Variant/Sub-type 43
- **Category:** Chronic / Respiratory
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Chronic airway inflammation, hyperresponsiveness, and bronchoconstriction. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Wheezing, shortness of breath, chest tightness, nighttime coughing fits. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Spirometry (FEV1/FVC ratio), Peak Expiratory Flow (PEF) monitoring. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Inhaled Corticosteroids (ICS) for control, Short-Acting Beta-Agonists (SABA) like Albuterol for rescue. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Avoiding known triggers (pollen, dust mites, smoke), annual vaccination. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 44. Disease: Coronary Artery Disease (CAD) - Variant/Sub-type 44
- **Category:** Chronic / Cardiovascular
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Atherosclerotic plaque accumulation in coronary arteries. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.

- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Antiplatelets (Aspirin), Statins (Atorvastatin), Beta-blockers, Angioplasty (stenting), CABG surgery. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Smoking cessation, low-sodium/low-fat diet, managing cholesterol and blood pressure. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 45. Disease: Chronic Obstructive Pulmonary Disease (COPD) - Variant/Sub-type 45
- **Category:** Chronic / Respiratory
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Long-term inflammatory response in lungs, typically due to smoking, causing alveolar destruction. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Chronic cough with sputum, progressive dyspnea (breathlessness), wheezing, chest tightness. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Spirometry showing post-bronchodilator FEV1/FVC < 0.70. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Smoking cessation, bronchodilators (LAMA, LABA), inhaled steroids, pulmonary rehabilitation, oxygen therapy. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Avoid smoking and exposure to occupational dust, chemicals, and biomass smoke. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 46. Disease: Hypothyroidism - Variant/Sub-type 46
- **Category:** Chronic / Endocrine
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Insufficient production of thyroid hormones (T3 and T4) by the thyroid gland. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Weight gain, fatigue, cold intolerance, dry skin, constipation, bradycardia, depression. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Serum TSH test (elevated), Free T4 levels (lowered). Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Daily oral administration of synthetic thyroid hormone (Levothyroxine). In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Ensuring adequate iodine intake (via iodized salt), regular thyroid screening in risk groups. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 47. Disease: Gastroesophageal Reflux Disease (GERD) - Variant/Sub-type 47
- **Category:** Chronic / Digestive
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Dysfunctional lower esophageal sphincter (LES) allowing gastric acid reflux. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Heartburn, acid regurgitation, dysphagia, chronic dry cough, chest pain. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Clinical history, Upper Endoscopy, 24-hour esophageal pH monitoring. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Proton Pump Inhibitors (Omeprazole), H2 blockers (Famotidine), antacids, elevating head during sleep. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Avoiding trigger foods (fatty, spicy, caffeine), eating smaller meals, avoiding lying down after eating. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 48. Disease: Osteoporosis - Variant/Sub-type 48
- **Category:** Chronic / Musculoskeletal
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Imbalance between bone resorption and bone formation, leading to decreased bone density. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Often silent until a fracture occurs, loss of height, stooped posture (dowager's hump). Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Dual-Energy X-ray Absorptiometry (DEXA) scan showing T-score <= -2.5. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Calcium and Vitamin D supplements, Bisphosphonates (Alendronate), weight-bearing exercise. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Adequate calcium intake during youth, smoking cessation, limiting alcohol, regular exercise. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 49. Disease: Chronic Kidney Disease (CKD) - Variant/Sub-type 49
- **Category:** Chronic / Renal
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Gradual loss of renal function due to long-term damage (mostly from diabetes and hypertension). This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Fatigue, edema (fluid retention in legs), uremic frost, nausea, anemia, decreased urine output. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Glomerular Filtration Rate (eGFR) < 60 mL/min for 3+ months, urine albumin-to-creatinine ratio (UACR). Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Tight control of blood pressure/blood sugar, ACE inhibitors, diuretics, dialysis, kidney transplantation. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Early screening of diabetic/hypertensive patients, avoiding nephrotoxic drugs (NSAIDs). Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 50. Disease: Rheumatoid Arthritis (RA) - Variant/Sub-type 50
- **Category:** Chronic / Autoimmune
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Autoimmune destruction of synovial joints leading to chronic synovitis. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Symmetrical joint pain and swelling (particularly hands/feet), morning stiffness lasting > 1 hour, fatigue. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Rheumatoid Factor (RF), Anti-CCP antibodies, ESR, C-Reactive Protein (CRP), joint X-rays. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Disease-Modifying Antirheumatic Drugs (DMARDs like Methotrexate), Biologics, NSAIDs, physical therapy. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Early diagnosis and treatment to prevent permanent joint deformity and bone erosion. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 51. Disease: Alzheimer's Disease - Variant/Sub-type 51
- **Category:** Neurological
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Accumulation of amyloid-beta plaques and neurofibrillary tangles in the brain. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Progressive memory loss, disorientation, difficulty planning, mood fluctuations, loss of autonomy. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Clinical evaluation, cognitive tests (MMSE), brain MRI/PET scans, CSF biomarkers. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Cholinesterase inhibitors (Donepezil), NMDA receptor antagonists (Memantine), monoclonal antibodies (Lecanemab). In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Mental stimulation, physical exercise, healthy cardiovascular diet (Mediterranean diet). Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 52. Disease: Parkinson's Disease - Variant/Sub-type 52

- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Loss of dopamine-producing neurons in the substantia nigra. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.

Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Levodopa-Carbidopa, Dopamine agonists (Pramipexole), MAO-B inhibitors, Deep Brain Stimulation (DBS). In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Regular exercise, minimizing exposure to pesticides, maintaining cognitive health. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 53. Disease: Epilepsy - Variant/Sub-type 53
- **Category:** Neurological
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Abnormal, hypersynchronous electrical activity in brain neurons. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Recurrent, unprovoked seizures, temporary confusion, staring spells, uncontrollable jerking movements. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Electroencephalogram (EEG), Brain MRI, video-EEG monitoring. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Anti-Seizure Medications (ASMs like Levetiracetam, Carbamazepine, Valproic Acid), ketogenic diet, vagus nerve stimulation. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Avoiding head injuries, proper prenatal care, avoiding triggers like sleep deprivation. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 54. Disease: Major Depressive Disorder (MDD) - Variant/Sub-type 54
- **Category:** Psychiatric
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Monoamine neurotransmitter dysregulation and neuroendocrine changes. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Persistent sadness, anhedonia (loss of interest), fatigue, changes in sleep/appetite, feelings of worthlessness. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** DSM-5 diagnostic criteria based on clinical interview, screening questionnaires (PHQ-9). Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Antidepressants (SSRIs like Sertraline, SNRIs), Psychotherapy (CBT), electroconvulsive therapy (ECT) for severe cases. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Stress management, maintaining social connections, early psychological intervention. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 56. Disease: Schizophrenia - Variant/Sub-type 56
- **Category:** Psychiatric
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Dopaminergic and glutamatergic neurotransmission abnormalities. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Delusions, hallucinations (auditory), disorganized speech, flat affect, social withdrawal. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Clinical psychiatric evaluation, ruling out organic brain pathologies via MRI and toxicology. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Antipsychotic medications (Risperidone, Olanzapine, Clozapine), psychosocial rehabilitation. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Early intervention during prodromal phase, avoiding substance abuse (cannabis increases risk). Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 57. Disease: Stroke (Ischemic/Hemorrhagic) - Variant/Sub-type 57
- **Category:** Neurological
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Interrupted blood flow to brain tissue causing rapid neuronal death. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Sudden numbness or weakness in face/arm/leg, difficulty speaking (aphasia), confusion, loss of balance. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Non-contrast Brain CT scan (to differentiate ischemic vs hemorrhagic), Brain MRI. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Thrombolytic therapy (tPA) for ischemic stroke within 4.5 hours, surgical decompression for hemorrhagic stroke. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Managing hypertension, smoking cessation, anticoagulants for atrial fibrillation, active lifestyle. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

---

### 58. Disease: Migraine - Variant/Sub-type 58
- **Category:** Neurological
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Trigeminal nerve activation and release of vasoactive neuropeptides like CGRP. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Unilateral pulsating headache, moderate to severe intensity, nausea, photophobia, phonophobia, aura in some cases. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Clinical history and diagnostic criteria (IHS), ruling out secondary headaches. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Triptans (Sumatriptan), CGRP antagonists, NSAIDs, preventive drugs (Beta-blockers, Topiramate). In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Identifying and avoiding triggers (specific foods, stress, sleep changes, bright lights). Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 59. Disease: Multiple Sclerosis (MS) - Variant/Sub-type 59
- **Category:** Neurological / Autoimmune
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Demyelination of central nervous system axons mediated by auto-reactive T-cells. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Visual disturbances (optic neuritis), muscle weakness, paresthesia (numbness/tingling), ataxia, fatigue. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Brain/Spine MRI showing demyelinating plaques, Lumbar puncture (Oligoclonal bands in CSF). Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Disease-Modifying Therapies (Ocrelizumab, Interferon-beta), corticosteroids for acute relapses. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Adequate Vitamin D intake, smoking cessation, regular medical follow-ups. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 60. Disease: Bipolar Disorder - Variant/Sub-type 60

- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Genetic predisposition and neurotransmitter receptor sensitivity fluctuations. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Maintaining consistent sleep cycles, avoiding alcohol and recreational drugs, medication compliance. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 61. Disease: Influenza (Seasonal Flu) - Variant/Sub-type 61
- **Category:** Infectious (Viral)
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Influenza Virus (Type A, B, C). This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** High fever, sore throat, muscle aches, severe fatigue, dry cough. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Rapid Influenza Diagnostic Tests (RIDT), RT-PCR nasal swabs. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Rest, hydration, antivirals (Oseltamivir/Tamiflu), NSAIDs for fever. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Annual influenza vaccination, hand hygiene, avoiding close contact. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 62. Disease: Tuberculosis (TB) - Variant/Sub-type 62
- **Category:** Infectious (Bacterial)
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Mycobacterium tuberculosis. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Chronic productive cough (sometimes blood-stained), night sweats, weight loss, low-grade fever. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Mantoux Tuberculin Skin Test (TST), Sputum smear microscopy, GeneXpert, Chest X-ray. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Combination antibiotic therapy (Isoniazid, Rifampicin, Pyrazinamide, Ethambutol) for 6-9 months. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** BCG vaccination, proper ventilation, isolation of active cases. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 63. Disease: Malaria - Variant/Sub-type 63
- **Category:** Infectious (Parasitic)
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Plasmodium species (P. falciparum, P. vivax). This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Cyclical high fever, chills, profuse sweating, headache, hemolytic anemia, splenomegaly. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Thick and thin blood smears under light microscopy, Rapid Diagnostic Tests (RDTs). Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Artemisin-based Combination Therapy (ACT), Chloroquine, Primaquine. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Insecticide-treated bed nets, indoor residual spraying, prophylactic medications. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 64. Disease: Cholera - Variant/Sub-type 64
- **Category:** Infectious (Bacterial)
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Vibrio cholerae. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Profuse watery diarrhea (rice-water stools), severe vomiting, rapid dehydration, muscle cramps. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Stool culture on TCBS agar, rapid dipstick tests. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Aggressive oral rehydration therapy (ORT), intravenous fluids (Ringer's Lactate), antibiotics (Doxycycline). In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Clean drinking water, proper sanitation, oral cholera vaccines (OCVs). Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 66. Disease: Dengue Fever - Variant/Sub-type 66
- **Category:** Infectious (Viral)
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Dengue Virus (DENV 1-4) transmitted by Aedes mosquitoes. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Sudden high fever, severe headache, retro-orbital pain, muscle and joint pain (breakbone fever), rash. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** NS1 antigen ELISA, IgM/IgG antibody tests, RT-PCR. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Symptomatic treatment, hydration, paracetamol (avoid aspirin/NSAIDs due to bleeding risk). In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Vector control, eliminating stagnant water, insect repellents, mosquito nets. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 67. Disease: Hepatitis B - Variant/Sub-type 67

- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Hepatitis B Virus (HBV). This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.

Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Hepatitis B vaccination within 24 hours of birth, safe needle practices, screen blood donations. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 70. Disease: Rabies - Variant/Sub-type 70
- **Category:** Infectious (Viral)
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Rabies Lyssavirus (transmitted via saliva of infected animals). This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Hydrophobia, aerophobia, hallucinations, hyper-salivation, paralysis, near 100% lethality once symptoms manifest. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Direct Fluorescent Antibody (DFA) test in animal brain, RT-PCR in humans. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Immediate wound washing, Post-Exposure Prophylaxis (PEP) with Rabies Vaccine and Immunoglobulin. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Pre-exposure vaccination of domestic pets, avoiding contact with wild animals. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 71. Disease: Diabetes Mellitus (Type 2) - Variant/Sub-type 71
- **Category:** Chronic / Metabolic
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Insulin resistance and relative insulin deficiency. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Polydipsia (excessive thirst), polyuria (frequent urination), polyphagia (hunger), slow wound healing. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Fasting Plasma Glucose (FPG) >= 126 mg/dL, HbA1c >= 6.5%, Oral Glucose Tolerance Test (OGTT). Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Metformin, sulfonylureas, GLP-1 receptor agonists, insulin injections, low-carb diet, exercise. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Maintaining a healthy body weight, active lifestyle, balanced diet rich in fiber. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 72. Disease: Hypertension (High Blood Pressure) - Variant/Sub-type 72
- **Category:** Chronic / Cardiovascular
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Increased systemic vascular resistance and arterial stiffness. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Often asymptomatic (the silent killer), headache, dizziness, tinnitus, chest pressure. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Repeated blood pressure readings >= 130/80 mmHg using a sphygmomanometer. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Lifestyle changes (DASH diet, reduced sodium), ACE inhibitors, Beta-blockers, Calcium channel blockers. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Regular physical activity, reducing sodium intake, stress management, limiting alcohol. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 73. Disease: Asthma - Variant/Sub-type 73
- **Category:** Chronic / Respiratory
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Chronic airway inflammation, hyperresponsiveness, and bronchoconstriction. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Wheezing, shortness of breath, chest tightness, nighttime coughing fits. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Spirometry (FEV1/FVC ratio), Peak Expiratory Flow (PEF) monitoring. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Inhaled Corticosteroids (ICS) for control, Short-Acting Beta-Agonists (SABA) like Albuterol for rescue. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Avoiding known triggers (pollen, dust mites, smoke), annual vaccination. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 74. Disease: Coronary Artery Disease (CAD) - Variant/Sub-type 74
- **Category:** Chronic / Cardiovascular
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Atherosclerotic plaque accumulation in coronary arteries. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Angina (chest pain radiating to arm/jaw), shortness of breath, myocardial infarction (heart attack). Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.

Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Antiplatelets (Aspirin), Statins (Atorvastatin), Beta-blockers, Angioplasty (stenting), CABG surgery. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Smoking cessation, low-sodium/low-fat diet, managing cholesterol and blood pressure. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 75. Disease: Chronic Obstructive Pulmonary Disease (COPD) - Variant/Sub-type 75
- **Category:** Chronic / Respiratory
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Long-term inflammatory response in lungs, typically due to smoking, causing alveolar destruction. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Chronic cough with sputum, progressive dyspnea (breathlessness), wheezing, chest tightness. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Spirometry showing post-bronchodilator FEV1/FVC < 0.70. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Smoking cessation, bronchodilators (LAMA, LABA), inhaled steroids, pulmonary rehabilitation, oxygen therapy. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Avoid smoking and exposure to occupational dust, chemicals, and biomass smoke. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 76. Disease: Hypothyroidism - Variant/Sub-type 76
- **Category:** Chronic / Endocrine
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Insufficient production of thyroid hormones (T3 and T4) by the thyroid gland. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Weight gain, fatigue, cold intolerance, dry skin, constipation, bradycardia, depression. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Serum TSH test (elevated), Free T4 levels (lowered). Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Daily oral administration of synthetic thyroid hormone (Levothyroxine). In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Ensuring adequate iodine intake (via iodized salt), regular thyroid screening in risk groups. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

---

### 77. Disease: Gastroesophageal Reflux Disease (GERD) - Variant/Sub-type 77
- **Category:** Chronic / Digestive
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Dysfunctional lower esophageal sphincter (LES) allowing gastric acid reflux. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Heartburn, acid regurgitation, dysphagia, chronic dry cough, chest pain. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Clinical history, Upper Endoscopy, 24-hour esophageal pH monitoring. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Proton Pump Inhibitors (Omeprazole), H2 blockers (Famotidine), antacids, elevating head during sleep. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Avoiding trigger foods (fatty, spicy, caffeine), eating smaller meals, avoiding lying down after eating. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 78. Disease: Osteoporosis - Variant/Sub-type 78
- **Category:** Chronic / Musculoskeletal
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Imbalance between bone resorption and bone formation, leading to decreased bone density. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Often silent until a fracture occurs, loss of height, stooped posture (dowager's hump). Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Dual-Energy X-ray Absorptiometry (DEXA) scan showing T-score <= -2.5. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Calcium and Vitamin D supplements, Bisphosphonates (Alendronate), weight-bearing exercise. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Adequate calcium intake during youth, smoking cessation, limiting alcohol, regular exercise. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 79. Disease: Chronic Kidney Disease (CKD) - Variant/Sub-type 79
- **Category:** Chronic / Renal
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Gradual loss of renal function due to long-term damage (mostly from diabetes and hypertension). This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Fatigue, edema (fluid retention in legs), uremic frost, nausea, anemia, decreased urine output. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Glomerular Filtration Rate (eGFR) < 60 mL/min for 3+ months, urine albumin-to-creatinine ratio (UACR). Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Tight control of blood pressure/blood sugar, ACE inhibitors, diuretics, dialysis, kidney transplantation. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Early screening of diabetic/hypertensive patients, avoiding nephrotoxic drugs (NSAIDs). Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 80. Disease: Rheumatoid Arthritis (RA) - Variant/Sub-type 80
- **Category:** Chronic / Autoimmune
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Autoimmune destruction of synovial joints leading to chronic synovitis. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Symmetrical joint pain and swelling (particularly hands/feet), morning stiffness lasting > 1 hour, fatigue. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Rheumatoid Factor (RF), Anti-CCP antibodies, ESR, C-Reactive Protein (CRP), joint X-rays. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Disease-Modifying Antirheumatic Drugs (DMARDs like Methotrexate), Biologics, NSAIDs, physical therapy. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Early diagnosis and treatment to prevent permanent joint deformity and bone erosion. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 81. Disease: Alzheimer's Disease - Variant/Sub-type 81
- **Category:** Neurological
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Accumulation of amyloid-beta plaques and neurofibrillary tangles in the brain. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Progressive memory loss, disorientation, difficulty planning, mood fluctuations, loss of autonomy. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Clinical evaluation, cognitive tests (MMSE), brain MRI/PET scans, CSF biomarkers. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Cholinesterase inhibitors (Donepezil), NMDA receptor antagonists (Memantine), monoclonal antibodies (Lecanemab). In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Mental stimulation, physical exercise, healthy cardiovascular diet (Mediterranean diet). Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 82. Disease: Parkinson's Disease - Variant/Sub-type 82
- **Category:** Neurological
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Loss of dopamine-producing neurons in the substantia nigra. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Resting tremor, bradykinesia (slowness of movement), rigidity, postural instability, micrographic writing. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Clinical neurological exam, DaTscan imaging, positive response to levodopa medication. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Levodopa-Carbidopa, Dopamine agonists (Pramipexole), MAO-B inhibitors, Deep Brain Stimulation (DBS). In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Regular exercise, minimizing exposure to pesticides, maintaining cognitive health. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.

### 83. Disease: Epilepsy - Variant/Sub-type 83
- **Category:** Neurological
- **Etiology & Pathophysiology:** Abnormal, hypersynchronous electrical activity in brain neurons. This condition represents a significant disruption to physiological homeostasis. At the cellular level, the biological mechanisms involve cellular damage, inflammatory cascades, and organ system dysfunction. Over time, if left unaddressed, the chronic strain on the body can lead to secondary complications, systemic collapse, or irreversible cellular remodeling.
- **Clinical Signs & Symptoms:** Recurrent, unprovoked seizures, temporary confusion, staring spells, uncontrollable jerking movements. Patients may also present with secondary symptoms including localized inflammation, fatigue, fever, and changes in metabolic biomarkers. The onset can range from acute and sudden to insidious and slowly progressive, depending on the patient's age, immune response, and overall health status.
- **Diagnostic Methodologies:** Electroencephalogram (EEG), Brain MRI, video-EEG monitoring. Differential diagnosis is critical to exclude overlapping clinical syndromes. Physicians combine laboratory blood profiles, microbiological cultures, and advanced imaging modalities to construct a definitive diagnostic profile.
- **Pharmacotherapy & Clinical Management:** Anti-Seizure Medications (ASMs like Levetiracetam, Carbamazepine, Valproic Acid), ketogenic diet, vagus nerve stimulation. In addition to primary medical therapy, clinical management requires regular patient monitoring, dietary adjustments, and physical rehabilitation. Dosages and drug selections must be tailored to prevent side effects and drug-to-drug interactions, particularly in elderly or comorbid patients.
- **Prevention & Public Health Measures:** Avoiding head injuries, proper prenatal care, avoiding triggers like sleep deprivation. Public health guidelines emphasize community-level education, routine screening, and sanitary standards to reduce transmission and incidence. Early identification remains the single most effective method to improve clinical outcomes and reduce healthcare burdens.
